RATING: 5
Chaotic negotiation game with significant random elements, and a decent theme.

Each round, cards (store locations) and then tiles (stores) are randomly distributed to each player, and then players essentially negotiate for anything and everything (stores, store locations, money), in an effort to make the largest groupings of stores together, in order to achieve the ultimate goal of most money (larger grouped stores equals more money each round).  Pure negotiation chaos, which makes the game very unique, but necessarily great.   Can be frustrating if your store tiles and/or locations are in bad spots- true you can often turn them into valuable items for others, but sometimes there's just nothing that can be done.  There are also random event cards drawn at the end of each round, which can have a not unsignificant impact on income.  All in all, an interesting but somewhat overrated game that probably isn't worth the $100 or so you'd have to pay to obtain a copy.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
*Rating based on one play.

This older title is almost pure negotiation.  There's just enough to the game to make things to negotiate over, but the meat of the game is in the negotiation.  For that reason, it's a highly interactive game, but not in a take-that kind of way.  Rather, it's almost semi-cooperative in that you have to think about what the other players want in order to make appealing offers to them, or at least be able to convince them that a deal is good for them.  I enjoyed this type of interaction.

However, I feel the game is just a bit too simple for my taste.  I would have liked a few more layers of depth to it.  I also felt that the end game was rather disappointing.  Maybe it was just the way our first play shook out, but there were almost no trades in the final round, which felt anti-climactic.  I feel like there needs to be more of a ramp in income for larger, incomplete businesses.  As it was, in the final round, my options were limited to playing out my three tiles for $1,000 each, or buying a matching one for an extra $2,000, but at the cost of $1,000 for the tile and the loss of $1,000 for having an extra tile I couldn't play.  The other players were largely in the same position, so the last round was very unexciting.

In the end, I felt it was a decent game, but felt it had some room for improvement.

RATING: 8
FAMILY, STRATEGY

Develop wealth in Chinatown (in New York) by bargaining (trading) for land and businesses with other players. Player with the most cash after six "years" (rounds) of play wins! A highly interactive game.

This game can be a real nail-biter. In one game, only $10,000 (the smallest denomination) separated Nick and me. 

Recommended with 4-5 players (best 5). 60 mins.

I owned the Filosofia German edition of the game.  It's quite nicely produced with decent artwork, although the bits do  rattle around in a great big box.

RATING: 7
This is almost a pure negotiation game.  As a rule I'm pretty good at these types of games.  For example I've won well over half of my Bohnanza plays, despite the fact that the typical game has at least 5 players.  I've only played Chinatown twice, both times mostly against players who've had much more experience with the game than me, and I've won both times.  On reflection I think this is due to two things.  With negotiation games, despite the fact that I am otherwise fairly introverted, I go out of my way to make deals, and I have an intuitive feel for exactly when to drive or not drive a hard bargain.  I like Chinatown, though don't know that I'd ever ask to play it.  Z-man's edition is visually very attractive, but I have to be honest, the box is just full of air and the retail price is a bit high for what you get in terms of components.

RATING: 7
An interesting blend of pure negotiation and high luck elements.  The random draw of the land plots and the business tiles makes the game highly luck dependent.  This is off-set by the amount of negotiation that the game encourages.  Effective trading will lead to better results, but you most be good at negotiation to convince people to "let you win".  The drawback to the design is that if a player gets great draws and doesn't need to negotiate, it can be very tough to win.  [GTI Code: AGCD]

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Chinatown fills by itself a niche in the hobby: it is a light weight pure negotiation game.

Many parts of its design are potentially bad, especially when seen with the eyes of the modern boardgamer: the game is full of randomness, but after the district card have been dealt, all the information is open and therefore each deal is calculable. And there are issues with potential runaway leaders too...
But in the end the game is fun, the negotiation that ensues is very thematic, and the relative short playtime makes the game don't overstay its welcome.

I thought I didn't enjoy pure negotiation games, but Chinatown made me change my mind!

RATING: 8
Initial Impression: What a great game! The rules are super simple and this has to be one of the easiest games I've ever taught. The gameplay is quick, with only 6 turns, and each phase of a turn is short and sweet. The main mechanism, the negotiation, is done just right as people aren't hindered by constraints. There are just the right number of resources to negotiate with and even AP prone players will be able to keep up with the wheeling and dealing. I can see this getting to the table for many more plays.

RATING: 4
Played once as a 5-player game with the rules explained to me.  Keep in mind that I hate negotiation games which feature the random distribution of resources.  Anyway, Chinatown allows players to negotiate anything in their possession for as long as they want to continue talking.  I usually do not have the stamina for these types of games for the trading rounds take forever when people are trying to get something from one person so that they can trade it and something else to another person, but more than one person is trying to do the same thing... After five minutes of this I endup withdrawing and hoping for the trading rounds to end as soon as possible.  

The components are fantastic!  The money is the best paper money that I have ever seen in gaming.

RATING: 3
Real disappointment - feels a bit like Acquire with trading thrown in. Downtime is horrible, listening to others arguing over who should get what gets tedious fast, deals between 2 people are okay, but when it involves 3 or 4, or is dependant on other trades happening (so you have chains) whole thing drags, would hate to play this with a slow player! 

Also there's no real balance mechanism for the rich getting richer - if I have a tile you want and I'm ahead of you why would I trade? Only if I'm forcing you to a deal that helps me more than you. Of course people behind the leader need to trade to catch up. There's too much open information, yes cash is secret but it's not hard to track, especially as income is open and deals are open too.

Was desperate to get this, having played it now I'm not!!

RATING: 7
First play of this game I own, with 5 people. Upon reading the rules I was afraid luck of the draw would factor too heavily into who might win, but this wasn't the case. The other thing I thought upon reading the rules was this was a streamlined game without a lot of finnicky side rules or exceptions, and that was absolutely true. Nice.

We played with a 3-minute timer on negotiating, and it was a fun game with lots of deals and swings in who seemed to be was winning. I held out giving one player the key piece he needed to complete a 6-store set until the last turn of the game (when he could do the same for me), and that was the difference that let me win this game. Will be interesting to play again; I could see this going up to a 7.5 or maybe even an 8.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
A very fun, if mildly unsatisfying game. The constant negotiation is really interesting, and reminds me a lot of Bohnanza, except in Chinatown you might actively pass on taking something that could benefit you in favor of taking something that you know your opponents really want. Bargaining power is ultimate power in this game, which is really neat, and makes this game unlike any other that I own.

Unfortunately, you still can't get around the luck factor: drawing the right cards or combinations of tiles can simply favor one player over another, and no amount of cajoling or begging on the part of the other players can change that. Of course, you can refuse to trade with someone in the lead, but often you're so limited yourself that you need their help more than they need yours. So, ultimately it's a fun game, but maybe not a very good one.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Excellent negotiation game, but it can take a while since calculating the value of the deals isn't hard (just time consuming).  Luck of the draw plays a larger role than I like.  If you draw lots that are far away from the other players, or businesses that nobody needs at the moment, you aren't involved in the deals and your position stagnates.  Add to this that not all lots and buildings come out during the game and you can end up in a position where your best investment options are never available.  Sure, you can (and must) account for this as you play, but this style of game makes it particularly painful to lose based on luck (bad rolls in a dice game = expected; not drawing anything of value in a negotiation game = sucks).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Chinatown is the best negotiation game I have found so far. There are just enough rules to provide a framework for the haggling, but the free-for-all negotiation is a blast. Very, very interactive, especially when there is no true structure to the trading.... anything goes. This type of game might be a turnoff for some, but as long as everyone is not a total introvert, or at least has 10% of a used-car salesman inside them... this game will help them discover it.

If every negotiation game needs a "driver" to generate the haggling, Chinatown's drivers are the need to collect certain tiles, followed by the need to gather the adjacent spots to place those tiles. There's never any downtime in Chinatown, as the round ends when everyone decides it should end. There are strong motivations for negotiating.

Another perk is that Chinatown is truly a gateway game. It's very accessible for new gamers, and the incredibly attractive Z-Man Games edition adds to the pull for newbies. There is a single but important nitpick--I can see the merit to critiques about the endgame grinding to a halt if gamers start to "do the math" in the final rounds, but the remedy is to pick the right players, and possibly, to introduce the optional bonus cards that dole out a little extra cash for certain building sizes. I'm still convinced this is the ideal entry-level negotiation game.

RATING: 9
Chinatown is a game where you are a Chinese immigrant in America 1960 something and you are trying to make your business bloom by building shops and making deals with other Chinese immigrants. The one who have the most money in the end RULES CHINATOWN WITH AN IRON GRIP!!! or that's what i like to think.  

Chinatown is as the subtitle say a game about negotiation. If you look in wikipedia it says "Negotiation is a dialogue between two or more people or parties intended to reach a beneficial outcome", this game is not about two parties getting a benefical outcome.
This game, in the deep of it, is about mind games, tricking your opponent into thinking it was a good deal by using false logic and math. 

And the feeling, knowing that you have tricked your friend giving you their hard earned money to you in a desperate move you improve their income but you have really just lured them into a economic trap where you have the leverage, is goooood. 

As you might have guessed this is not a friendly game. 
You can play it as one but it would be a boring variant of..well.. life. 
This is you taking up the role as a cutthroat angry  Chinese immigrant willing to kill (no you cannot do that in  this game) for more money and more power. 

This game is not something i bring every game night, but it appears about 6-8 times ayear on the table. 
It is easy you teach and takes about 1-2 hours with 5 people. 
I could teach my 5 year old son this game but it would probably end up with me stealing all his money and dancing. 

This game is hubris in a box and i love it!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 2
Chinatown is a game with way too much open information and it additionally, like most trading/negotiation games, depends heavily the personality of the group you're playing it with for maximum enjoyment.

The basic idea behind this game is pretty simple: you want to control blocks of businesses on a board depicting Chinatown. The business tiles are randomly drawn, as are the lot ownership rights, and both of these can then be swapped about freely during the trading phase of the game, which is the real meat of the game. Players ideally want to get connected lines of like businesses, with the rarer business types paying out larger returns if you get a complete "set".

I played it with a group of 5 people, which seems like it would be the best scale, but the luck of the draw, though fixable by dealing, seems a little too dominant in dictating who has the upper hand. Chinatown felt like a math puzzle at some level as well. The game should be fun for its free-wheeling haggleathons, but everything ended up feeling too calculable which really stifles negotiations.

The "I've got what you need and refuse to deal with you" aspect is also a large one for me, if someone is in the lead and you're in second, and they draw the last tile you need there is no way to get it away from them. The real killer though is the nigh completely open information, it is way too easy to calculate the value of a given trade out, and hence, negotiations frequently end up stalling out. The only place you can get ahead against savvy opponents is in the draw, which isn't exactly a mark of distinction for me. Calculable information can be OK, but I think that it has to be transitory, i.e.: there can be events that occur which change that value significantly. This is why, for instance, the calculations of Modern Art work for me (you don't know when the round is going to end, you don't know if the painting is going to maintain its current value) and why games like Container and Chinatown don't...you know what the stuff is worth, flat out, and it's just a question of trying to get a fair cut of the action.

One of the early alea disappointments from back when the label was actually reliable. I had expected that its reputation would suffer noticeably when it was reprinted, but somehow this one continues to attract fans, and I can honestly say it's one of the few well-regarded games that I dislike where I can't even academically understand what people are seeing here that I'm not. I think Traders of Genoa is still and likely always will be my go-to in the pure negotiation genre. It has all of the haggling of this one, but adds a lot of closed information about the value of items which makes things move along much better.

RATING: 3
After playing I can't really imagine why people were pining after this game before it was republished. Massive sums of money exchanged hands... but after having played I conclude it has nothing to do with the qualities of the game and everything with a severe case of completitis—that nasty disease which has the infected person try to 'collect' as many things as possible from a series, against better judgment about the qualities of the things collected.

I perceive to be the main issue of the game that a deal's worth can be accurately calculated. Once players know how to do this quickly and accurately, Chinatown becomes a game of speculation against the luck of the draw stack and bag—my what interesting diversions. The only way to make this game work is by simply ignoring that you can calculate matters, but then I wonder what the purpose of the game is in the first place. If a trading game is going to work, values of lots must have different values to different people. 

I think I'm going to leave it at the first game.

An easy negotiation game to explain and play, plus, it's not so cutthroat as say Lifeboats or others. You also want to get properties/tiles valuable to your opponents, so you can sell them off or hold them hostage. That adds some nice interaction, dealings and timing.

RATING: 7
[2013-05-27 Rating: n.a. -> 7]
I didn't think that I would like a pure trading game, but Chinatown proved me wrong. I do seem to enjoy some negotiation under the right circumstances. Since I haven't a lot of games of Chinatown under the belt, here are just some observations. They might change with further plays: 

* The rules of the game are dead easy to explain. Setup and rules explanation will take five minutes at most. And this is a game that can be played with casual gamers.

* Chinatown isn't for everyone. I had some complaints from players who didn't like the open and chaotic trading phase. I guess, they would prefer a more regulated setting for their trades.

* Most of the values can be calculated during the last two rounds. So Chinatown can become a little mathy in these. I'm not sure yet if this might kill the fun in future games.

RATING: 5

I just find the negotiation aspect to be too open-ended for my tastes-- there's definitely room for creativity when coming up with deals, but it's a little exhausting.  I prefer the more limited trading that goes on in games like Settlers of Catan and Civilization, or the very structured trading systems of Basari and Res Publica.

RATING: 7
One of the best, when it comes to open negotiation. Since the trading phase is completely open, you get some really unusual deals all over the place, and it's hard to keep track of just how much money each person has. I'll always happily enter into a gamble deal: "I'll take your 2 restaurants, but I'll hand you any and all tea houses I draw for the rest of the game."

Game comes to it's weakest moment in the last two rounds - when you know that this action will literally give your opponent x money and make you y money, negotiation gets more difficult. Best if someone drives the game to its conclusion if people are getting caught up here.

RATING: 8.3
one of the requirements for a good social boardgame (in my humble opinion) is INTERACTION ... otherwise, we may as well sit in front of our computer playing 'Hearts' with three pseudo 'people' .. or have that ubiquitous pack of playing cards laying red nine on black ten ... Chinatown offers interaction in spades ... pure negotiation, where anything .. that is, anything, goes .. trade cash, lots, shoppe tiles, put together a package and make an offer no one can refuse .. sure you can determine how much money one is going to make in a round, and subsequent rounds, but, just what are they going to give up (how much cash are they including in their deals) to obtain that desired goal ... this is the ultimate in wheeling and dealing .. great no-holds barred action across the table , and a  game whereby you help others to help yourself .. the final round always tends to peter out ... but the action up until then, is absolute WCW Mayhem ... one of the best pure negotiation games out there, and a guaranteed good time, to shake those shy turtles out of their shells .. highly recommend ...

RATING: 9
Everyone likes, if not loves, this game; and I mean everyone! Even my dad, who hates board games, really got into the spirit of this one.  I do too, except it's the only game people want to play when I have them over.  The rules are simple: distribute, trade, build, make money.  It does a lot of the things Monopoly does but without the tedium.  I'll take a Dim Sum shop over Kentucky Avenue any day of the week. It's wheeling and dealing at its finest, and my gateway game of choice. Heck, it's one of my games of choice.

RATING: 6
[3 plays] This game is possibly more enjoyable than it deserves to be.  But I do have a couple of problems with it.  First, it sometimes feels like the game is herding us all toward the optimal-efficiency solution.  Second, things can bog down, especially in the late game, when people get really focused on calculating the exact value of trades to everyone. It really takes away the wheeling & dealing, negotiating flavor that it seems like this game should have, when every time you try to make a deal, the other party gets out their revenue card, methodically figures up exactly how much profit you'll make from the deal, and offers a price that takes it all away from you.  All this can be avoided by dealing more aggressively earlier in the game, before anyone can tell the value of anything for sure.  But that just makes the game more luck-dependent.  And you can end up feeling that you spent an elaborate 2 hours answering the question "Can I draw X tile from the bag right now, because if so I win, and if not I lose."

Maybe 6 rounds is a tad too long, players are doing the sums by the 5th round, but this is still a great game, with the winner often being a surprise. What I really like too is that the player left with several business tiles lying in front of it clearly cannot win, and deserves not to since they didn't trade them away.

Only had the opportunity to play Chinatown twice, in back to back games. I admit to falling in love with this game. So many crunchy decisions! So much angst! You may only succeed by jostling and elbowing your opponents as you fight your way to the victory. Lots of emerging gameplay in this title. CONS:

It works on that level, I guess. My [b]4th play[/b], I feel the same was as I did back in 2006. =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Chinatown is a free-form negotiation and trading game. Quite unusually the values of the properties that are haggled over are entirely transparent to the players. You know exactly what a certain lot or business is worth to another player - with the sole exception of random future draws which in some cases may or may not increase the value even further.

This is a huge problem. Without uncertainty about what other players can do with what you're giving away there is no game left. All it is about is whether or not people do the math to determine the tile values, and if everybody does that (and it's not like that's a difficult thing to do; you can mostly even read it right off the player aid), it comes down to who draws the more lucrative lots and businesses.

RATING: 8
[b]Chinatown[/b] [i]Karsten Hartwig 1999[/i]
Least ranked game in my collection but still the funniest trade game ever, pure negotiation in a quite easy game with tons of interactivity among players. I accept that Traders of Genoa is a bit better but Chinatown is far more accesible and after all a more funny game. Luck has a lot of importance and if you play with the wrong group it could end up being pretty boring, but with people who love this game and are good at trading and negotiation the game is a blast. It is also playable as a long filler and family game. I have the old ALEA edition which added a nice home rule that was forgotten in the new edition(connection to main streets necessary to start a new bussiness). All in all, a pretty good game that with the correct people turns out to be a fantastic experience.

RATING: 3
Chinatown is Sid Sackson's Metropolis with all the good parts removed. The only thing happening here is making property/business/cash deals with your opponents. That's fine, but it means that the game will succeed or fail based on the strength of the trading. And the trading stinks. Why? Because there's no hidden information, and it's extremely easy to calculate or at least approximate the expected value of any deal. Trading games work much better when there's some mystery about how much the "traded thing" is worth to different players. There's no mystery here. Thus no one overpays for anything, and what's left is a simple game of "trade or use everything in your possession and try to draw good tiles and cards." There's some interest in that, but mostly it's an un-fun game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
6/2/2013: My favorite games are pure negotiation games. I haven't played this yet, but I predict I will rate it a 9.

4/11/2015: Finally (!) played this. Yes, it's a fantastic game—free form trading is the best form of trading in life or in a game. 

It's nice because the game has a discrete end point (6 turns), which means you optimize your trading for the medium term. You can win by deal-making for capital assets and de-focusing on revenue streams. Of course, with a long enough time period, the players with more land and buildings would win, but 6 turns keeps the game more trade-oriented.

RATING: 7
Okay, negotiation games stress me out--how much is something worth to me or the other player? These types of games require assertiveness and a sense of brashness that are not present in most games. Chinatown has a lot of random elements, and the way the properties are acquired reminds me of Rhinelander. Placement of businesses is sort of like Acquire. However, the collecting and forming of sets of businesses reminds me of Monopoly. The theme is light, but the negotiating is heavy, sort of making this "Traders of Genoa-lite". Can be reasonably played in 45-60 minutes. Very easy to learn, but making the most of your negotiations is hard. I like this better than Genoa--less stressful. Rating: '7'.

RATING: 6
Since this game is pure negotiation it is much more player-dependent than many other games. Since I play with a rotating set of players that means this type of game doesn't really shine for me.

It doesn't help that my single player was ruined by a player who began playing irrationally part way through ("I'll give you all my money for that useless tile") which turned it into a battle for second place.

I'd like to give another try one day because I find the total lack of structure somewhat fascinating (i.e. people who refuse to trade for something that is clearly (to me, at least) in their best interest because they are paranoid or conservative or whatnot).

The total lack of structure means there is room for some creative trades but in practice they seem pretty uncommon -- but perhaps that is a consequence of "lack of consistent set of players".

RATING: 8.9
Feb10:

I had avoided it forever. Mainly due to the statements of "It's all calculable". And really, they are more right than wrong. You can really calculate out what many of the deals are really worth.

However, there are more that still have that air of intangible. I think I will play with your unplayed building tiles hidden next time. I just need to find some tile racks to fit in the box.

The recent printing was just gorgeous. It was really easy to teach and made perfect sense. We got going, and the negotiations started flying. The interaction was fun and jovial. We all threw on some wacky accents just because.

I would recommend a timer, 5 minutes worked for our 4 player game. I always like the idea of negotiation games, but so many of them seem to fall a little flat. Genoa is too long. Bohnanza is great, but a little light. Quo Vadis is solid, but too abstract for most people. I think Chinatown hits a happy medium.

RATING: 8
initial rating 2 plays 5 players....

Like any "real" trading or negotiation game this will be very group dependant. I found this to be a very unique game, it felt a little like aquire just due to placement and building buildings but it is completely different. A pure negotiation game through and through where almost everything is up for trade. The real unique thing I found is that there are no turns at all. People get their placements and their buildings, everyone flips over which buildings they have and then WHAM! The race is on to get the best trades possible done before someone else undercuts you. 

The placements and buildings are handed out randomly forcing you to make the best with what you have and almost ensuring the best negotiater will win as bad placements and buildings can be overcome through trading to a large degree. A real blast with the right group, 5 being best I think but 4 would be good too. Lots of yelling and laughing and 2 or 3 people trying to deal with one guy all at the same time. 

It's true all info is open and that could be somewhat modified but it worked great for our groups. Some deals went longer by trying to calculate but mostly things were done more hastily and that way is certainly more fun. The last 2 rounds things are definetly "easier" to calculate and then you might try to work out the value and split it in half with the other trader, this was fine too. Players of relatively equal skill will be important as a weak player could ruin deals or make too good of deals killing the balance.

Some luck, a little chaos and only a little strategy but great tactics and a lot of fun really make up for this. Overall a very fun title and hard to beat for pure negotiation, I prefer this to TOG as its much shorter and more "fun", if I want a longer more strategic nego game I'll go for TOG or Die Handler. If you're playing this slowly and calculating everything I believe you're not "playing" the way it was intended; fast, furious will be fun, any other way, not so much so won't be for everyone. If you want full on interaction this is it!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I love this game. The negotiations are really fun since everything is up for trade. There is almost no game mechanics here except for the end of round earnings from businesses. And here's the brilliance of the game, the entire game is driven by the players itself. Players create their own rules for trade with other players and determine the value of the trade. 

Be warned though, negotiations in this game can sometimes get out of hand and noisy so you might not want to play this in a place where your gaming group could stand out for being too loud. (kinda reminds me of those bargains and negotiations being done in a Chinese market). The game is also very heavily group dependant so gamers that don't like to negotiate may not like this game.

RATING: 7.2
Chinatown is a negotiation game where the principal game play mechanism is trading randomly drawn board locations and set collection chits with other players in an effort to generate the most recurring income.  The constant interaction makes the game fun for a group of shrewd negotiators.  There is a lot of randomness due to the manner in which resources are allocated among the players, but the ability to trade means that the randomness should not dictate the winner.

RATING: 7
In the increasingly crowded design space of modern board games, Chinatown occupies a place of its own as a lightweight pure negotiation game.  There is none of the delicious complexity of Genoa in this rule set, it's true, and normally that would be a turnoff to me.  Negotiation tends to insert a complexity of its own into any game which features it, though.

Relatively friendly to first-time players in that the valuations of most items are transparent.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Chinatown is an almost pure negotiation game. The board is made up of six grids of squares, each square on the board having a unique number. There is a deck of cards - one card for each square. There is also a bag of tiles with various businesses (Antiques, Restaurant, Photo, Take-Out, etc.) ranging in value from 3-6.  On each turn players draw cards and keep a few (depending on number of players and game phase) and claim ownership of the spaces they keep. Then business tiles are drawn from the bag (again, depending on # of players and game phase). Once drawn, the negotiations begin! Players can trade any number of spaces, tiles, cash, and so on to get the combos they want. Tiles are then placed. Adjacent tiles of matching businesses are worth more, when enough adjacent tiles are built to match the value number, that is considered a "complete" business and pays the most. Then income is paid out and you do it again. Whoever has the most money after 6 rounds wins.

Negotiations are obviously at the heart of the game. In the first and last rounds, the negotiations are weaker because the board is either too empty or too committed to do much, but the middle rounds are filled with negotiating possibilities. It seems quite possible that a player with a number of lucky draws could run away with the game without having to negotiate much. Whether or not you enjoy the game depends on how you like negotiation. If you love negotiation, there's a lot to like here. If you dislike negotiation there's almost nothing to like. One aspect that people may not enjoy is that it is very difficult to tell who is in the lead and by how much.

RATING: 6
A negotiation game.  My daughter and wife love this game, so I will never get rid of it.  Here's the thing.  At a certain point in the game, you can calculate what each tile is worth to you and your opponent.  That brings negotiation down to a screeching halt. Maybe my love for the game will go up if we begin playing at four players. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
[b]Read Full Comment for Death Spiral[/b]

Chinatown reminded me of Acquire, which I did not expect. You have fewer turns but draw more tiles from the bag. Instead of spending your money on stock, you can spend it negotiating with other players in order to get the tiles and spaces you want. Instead of big payoffs from mergers, you receive steady income from businesses, which ramps up when each business reaches a certain size. Everything is public, so you can see what you have that might be valuable to the other players and vice versa. You spend six turns making deals against the randomness of the draw and see who made the most money. I rather liked Chinatown. Does a lot with a little, and plays in about an hour.

Update (3 plays): Unfortunately, I think the first play is the best play, because people only have a decent feel for what they're doing, rather than an exacting approach that takes into account all future income. In the early game, there is some uncertainty about trading because you don't know who will draw what and change the picture. But even then, I find that players can be a bit disingenuous. I'm willing to articulate exactly what's at stake on either side of a trade. Other players tend to present fuzzy, incomplete, or irrelevant math. Rarely on purpose, of course, but still. 

Reading the comments on this game, my guess is that quite a few players don't/can't eyeball a trade and quickly determine it's value. For them, working through future income seems like some kind of lengthy analysis that goes against etiquette. I guess ignorance is bliss. 

I'm downgrading Chinatown to a 6. I think it's my first pure negotiation game, and there was a real spark for me before each trade involved the entire game-arc. It's sad that the first play is the best play. Definitely need to try Genoa. 

Final Update: My rating slides with each play, and I'm out of patience. I now strongly believe this to be a flawed or problematic design. During my last play, I actually watched the game get worse before my eyes as new players figured out how to calculate the value of tiles. For a brief moment, there's an illusion that you can make deals in good faith based more on a gut feeling of what it "seems" to be worth. Once that illusion is crushed, the game goes out the window. 

The main issue here is that [b]overwhelmingly[/b], people don't WANT the deal-making to be as deterministic as it is. So you basically have a camp of fans who outright ignore the way the game works, or who make a game out of misrepresenting the way the game works (swindling) and then you have everyone else who does the math. Once you've played a couple of times, I can't imagine that you'd want to be at the table in mixed company, regardless of your angle on the game. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6.5
I'm bumping up my initial "6" rating to a 6.5 after two widely separated plays. Chinatown is/can be fun, even uproarious, but, like most/all negotiating/tradiing/voting games, it is prone to, even predicated on, a metagame that not everyone will enjoy and that can easily leave someone on the outside for little reason other than interpersonal factors only semirelevant to the game. In my last play, the guy who probably played "best" in the abstract--was most aggressive with trade proposals, had the best sense of the tradeoffs--ended up coming in last because he came off too "hot" and made it so no one wanted to trade with him. Not a game I would recommend for every audience and situation, but, as advertised, it is almost pure negotiation, which is worth something.

I have the large-box Z-man edition from several years ago. I don't object to the size much, but I'm not a fan of the money, which comes in odd denominations that are easily confused for one another from a distance (because they're all green-on-white "simulations" of US bills). This may make money easier to hide, but it's also easy to make a mistake. I think I will trip poker chips next time even though it will be harder to keep the amounts secret.

RATING: 7
Like any other negotiation game, you have to make sure you're playing with an appropriate group for it to be any fun.  It's hard to not compare this to Traders of Genoa, since they're both Alea negotiation games.  I'd have to say that I like this a little more, mainly because of the shorter playing time.  I guess there are more options in ToG, and there's a huge amount of luck in Chinatown (because of random tile/card draws, which are much more important than the card draws in ToG), but it seems manageable.  I can see what others have said about being able to calculate what everything's worth, especially at the end, but that doesn't bother me too much.  I really enjoyed the game - the graphic design is pretty good and the board and pieces are attractive.  I'm not sure that this is worth the crazy prices it's going for, but I'm glad to own it and would gladly play it...with the right group, that is.  I didn't find the negotatiation aspect to be as aggressive as other games, however.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
What is with this game?

The sandbox negotiation round is about as fun as it gets in gameplay for me. It's wonderful and enjoyable, but the game has a serious flaw. Others have discussed it more eloquently, but the problem, ultimately, is that the more you play, the less enjoyable the game becomes. The game boils down to who can make the best and quickest calculations of the value of the deals in negotiation. That is what this game is -- who is better at mathematics.

Also, what is the incentive to take risks beyond a certain point in the game? One example: by the middle of the fourth round, I had three completed businesses on the board, passively earning me $270,000 per round. With two-and-a-half rounds left (more than a third of the game), why would I want to make any deals unless I drew something spectacular? And if I did draw something spectacular, why would anyone else want to deal with me?

Chinatown is a tremendous amount of fun -- I enjoy it -- but it is slightly flawed.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
This is a game of negotiation and trading. The goal is to make the most money in 6 rounds by building 'shops'. This involves getting ownership of adjacent locations on a grid and then getting matching sets of tiles into those locations which will earn money at the end of each round. 

This is the best negotiation game I've played. There are two things about this game that really stand out. I really like that there are three distinct but interconnected resources (tiles, locations, and money) and that you can make any sort of trade deal that you can manage involving any combination of resources (including your $'s, which are your victory points). I also like that you can deal with anyone and you can make deals involving multiple parties. It is basically completely open negotiation and trading which is refreshing and gives a sense of control and flexibility not found in other negotiation games. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 2
A pointless game.  The value of a given tile is fairly easy to determine.  The group determines the appropriate "price" for a given tile.  In our group it was 50% of the expected value, but it could be any arbitrary percentage.  The group will trend towards that percentage for all trades.  So it comes down to who gets better tiles.

There needs to be more ambiguity in the value of the tiles.  For example, "Modern Art" has a range of values, and it can be hard to determine that value.  A game like "Ra" makes it even more difficult to determine appropriate value.  Without ambiguity, the game is a simple mathematical exercise.

I guess if I was playing with mental weaklings, I could take advantage of their inability to make the appropriate calculations, but I played this game with strong players, which was, as I stated right at the beginning, completely pointless.

RATING: 5
I don't like full information negotiation games in general: since everything can be calculated, you are only negotiating in the hopes that somebody makes a mistake. Granted, you don't know which buildings / businesses are going to appear the next round, but that's just a layer of odds to add to your calculations.
Rather than all open information, I would've rather seen negotiations with non-symmetric information where different players know different things (see Hab & Gut for a non-negotiaton game using this mechanism). 

The new version looks gorgeous, but has one *gigantic* flaw: all the denominations look exactly alike apart from a very small number on the card. In a game where money is constantly changing hands this  means mistakes are bound to happen.

RATING: 6
This game is definitely showing its age.  The grid-like board is a little odd, and the fact that it's not a pure grid just means that the numbered plots can be harder to find.

It has a really simple rule set, and that's part of what is a bit frustrating about the experience -- it is difficult to teach people how to play it.

Also, you MUST play a game like this with the right people.  It is a trading/negotiation game.  As a result, it's hard to figure out the right group with which to get this to the table.

RATING: 8
An excellent, simple and elegant negotiation game.  I'd recommend this & Bohnanza for a light to mid-weight negotiation game with Traders of Genoa (also great) for a deeper negotiation/trading game.  I tend to favor this game with less than perfect information or a Hidden Goal variant (i.e. - end game bonus reward for businesses on a certain street), especially with serious players.  Another option is to keep tiles secret and reveal only when offering to trade or placing on the board.  This way, there is less perfect information.

RATING: 6
Finally got to play this classic negotiation game. Unfortunately for me, negotiation is one of my most disliked ingredients in a game. I don't like doing it. I'm not a dealmaker or a haggler. I'm a "let me make my purchase and be on my way" guy. A mark, if you will. In the real world I'll gladly take a price hit if it means ending the transaction sooner.

Chinatown, however, isn't too bad. Unlike Traders of Genoa, there's not a whole lot of faff on top of the trading and haggling that you also have to deal with. It's a lot more straightforward and, for me, easier to value the transactions. A lot of my gaming pals love Genoa, and while I certainly recognize it's a good game, it's not one I particularly enjoy playing. Chinatown, in my opinion, fills a very similar niche but with more zazz. I wonder if I can convert them?

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Chinatown has nothing much to do with Chinatown (the theme is completely bolted on) but everything to do with free trading. That's where the joy of this game resides: in all the sneaky, backstabbing, irrational deals that will keep happening, over and under the table. Of course, like all free form negotiation games, it needs the right group of players and there will be some opportunities for king making but you should nonetheless try it out. IMPORTANT INFORMATION: *keep the tiles and money you own hidden and lie through your teeth about them or deal values will be too transparent and suck the fun out!* Don't let the dry board fool you: Chinatown is lots of fun and a great game for conniving bastards. Intrige, however, remains the undisputed king of this category.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Chinatown is a negotiation game where you are randomly given city blocks and start with some money.  You are then allowed to make deals to buy or trade locations.  You then get business tiles that you can place.  You can buy, trade or sell business tiles from other players as well.  There is a final placement of tiles and an income phase.  

If you like light economic games with heavy negotiation this is for you.  

This is not a family weight game.  I would say it is just beyond family weight but still relatively light for most gamers.

RATING: 8.5
Played this on New Year's Eve 2015 with Erin's parents and my mother. It works well as a social/drinking game with an extremely simple rule set. It's sort of difficult to explain if you haven't played it before, but once you have, this game can be explained to newbies in 5 minutes. I like the bits (I really appreciate no paper money), and the plastic discs are preferable to cardboard chits, IMO. Depending on the group, I could see this game being anything from neatly regulated to total chaos. Our group was closer to the former, with a few rivalries being formed in the process. I felt like I had no chance of winning, but actually came out to being a close second because of a strong final round. I LOVE THIS GAME. If you like negotiation games, you need to have this in your collection. If you don't like negotiation games, this game probably won't change your mind. However, for what it's worth, it's the best pure negotiation game I've ever played.

After a second game, I can see how players can get screwed with bad location cards, and how others can get lucky with great location cards. In other words, if someone gets lucky by receiving great positions (positioned closely together) and is able to negotiate well, they are most likely going to beat a person who negotiates well, but doesn't get any good location cards. That's sort of unfortunate, but should be expected in this game. That doesn't take away from the game for me. Of course, the tile pulls are totally random too, but tiles seem to be easier to negotiate for than positions. I've lost both times I've played this game (mainly due to unfavorable location cards), and I still LOVE it! 

Update 3/21/15: Played with Haren, Blaine and Tyson. This was the first time to play with friends. The game had the the same sort of "serious" tension, which I like. It's not lighthearted, but it's still fun! After playing it with both family and friends, I don't really have a preference either way.

RATING: 7
OK negotiation game and can be an enjoyable game for non-gamers.  Chinatown was one of the first eurogames that I played way back in the early 2000s...

A problem I have with the game, is that it is relatively easy to calculate the expected future value of a trade due to the open information presented in the game (i.e. payoff matrix for groups of like business tiles).  The question then becomes, what is the "group think" discount rate and just how much of my gains do I have to give up to complete this trade?

This problem of readily calculated payoffs gets worse as the game nears the end.  So, fun first couple of plays, less fun thereafter.

RATING: 9
*

Awesome game of pure negotiation to get ahead.

Made a few modifications to the rules for sake of ease.  
-I teach the game where we do not shuffle the cards after each round.  Annoying to get the same card you just discarded in the previous round.  Only after all the cards have been played through do we shuffle the deck.
-Also, I teach the game to have everyone hold onto their property cards and trade them accordingly with negotiations instead of discarding them into the box.

RATING: 9
Chinatown is a super fabulous game about the bare essence of business and negotiation. Chinatown is a prime example of the technical term of "game theory" and waging the decision making questions: What happens if I take this action or make this offer, then what are the consequence? How will the other player react?  Chinatown is a great game of the high mindedness of negotiation. 

Warning:  do not play with "sensitive" players that could easily feel cheated during the negotiation  play. 

As of this commentary, the game is not in stock, but is expected to be in print in 2018. Check the publishers website. Beware of the high prices and fakes  in the secondary market.

RATING: 8
Chinatown is a functionally simple game. The board, cards, and tiles are really just to provide a framework and context for open negotiations, the heart of its gameplay. The free-form nature of the negotiations are similar to Bohnanza and the spirit of Cosmic Encounter, so if you dislike those games Chinatown is probably not for you.

ZMan's reissue is graphically a huge improvement over the Alea original. My only wish was that the game was set further in the past, somewhere near the end of the 19th century. Then it would have been more historically accurate.

Chinatown turned out to be everything I'd heard about it, which was exactly what I was looking for. Its so nice when something actually meets your expectations!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
The game does a great job at what it is, a simple negotiation game that relies on the basics, with no unnecesary complexities or adornments. The luck of the draw is mitigated and it is secondary to negotation. Still, you cannot negotiate out of certain bad luck scenarios.

With public information, it's very easy to calculate the benefit both parties will get from a certain exchange. That usually leads to split the benefits 50%-50% and means that those who are lucky enough to draw the most valued business tiles and places are potential winners.

Some time limit is needed for each negotiation round. 4-5 minutes worked fine for us.

The strategic/tactic content is very light. You can even talk about "push your luck" instead of tactic. You place tiles and do deals hoping that you will be able to draw or negociate similar tiles next to those, and you can take more or less risks.

On a personal level, I don't enjoy negotiating in real life, and also not in games. It's fun during some turns, but then I become bored of it. And I like it even less when you know the real price of whatever you're selling/buying (and you will with open information). I quickly become frustrated if I don't get the real value from something. I think I will enjoy it much more with secret information, but I don't know if the game will work that smoothly.

RATING: 8.5
Pretty much every aspect of Chinatown screams 'hate me' to the modern gamer. It's random, loud, chaotic and relies on a degree of co-operation not easily come by in my game group. There is potentially a ridiculous runaway leader problem, and players who take it too seriously may open a profit-loss book to the boredom of the rest of us.

But, from deep within this, comes the beauty of the theme. You can almost believe that all this shouting and chaos was how whole blocks of Chinatown were founded. There's certainly a great stock-market feel to the whole thing. And, out of the layers of ridiculousness, a great game is formed. Very much needs the right group to make it work, though.

RATING: 6
ZMan edition with very attractive bits. The new board is huge.

Player pay-out cards use red vs black text to distinguish different information. This is a stupid piece of design, but it's easy to create a colourblind version.

RATING: 8
[b]Chinatown[/b] was sold via auction 11/5/15.

[COLOR=#FF0000]A year later... thoughts and rating (8):[/COLOR]

[i]Yes I traded this game, but not because it isn't a good game. Essentially Lords of Vegas is a bigger hit with my family so Chinatown left our collection (although now that I'll have more space for games I may seek out another copy). What sealed the deal is we enjoy the amount of randomness introduced into LoV due to the takeovers and card flip (which determines payouts) so you never have perfect information. If you play Chinatown with the wrong crowd (well to me at least since I prefer playing fast and loose) Chinatown can devolve into a math exercise with people calculating exactly what their $ gains will since there is no way to mess with peoples buildings. So did it really stand the test of time? Since I would always be willing to play a game I'd say yes, just because a game is no longer in my collection doesn't mean I don't enjoy it.[/i]

[COLOR=#FF0000]Initial thoughts and rating (8):[/COLOR]

[i]- A pure negotiation game that works extremely well in a family setting.
- Very easy to teach, clean rule set and you are engaged the whole game (there isn't any down time).
- I will mention that the game can get pretty intense because everyone is negotiating at the same time. (We slowed it down a bit so my daughter (9) wouldn't get overwhelmed.)
- Production is good (although I'd prefer wooden discs to the plastic ones) and I appreciate the much smaller box.
- Not sure if I'd play this with less than 4, I think it would fall flat.

I was very happy when whole family enjoyed Chinatown. Up to this point we have played Lords of Vegas, but I'd been a bit weary of introducing a pure negotiation game because there isn't any luck involved, whereas in Lords of Vegas you have lots of die rolling which can swing the game in some unexpected ways. Because of that fact I definitely think there is room in our collection for both of these games, depending on what kind of mood we're in.

Both games are great fun, but the one thing Lords of Vegas has over Chinatown are those insane die rolls when everything is on the line and you could lose a huge casino to an opponent. However; I think Chinatown offers a more exciting negotiation environment because you are negotiating not only for the lots, but also the different business types which means you usually have something everyone else at the table wants.[/i]

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
心得:
需要嘴砲的遊戲
規則:
*商店丟進袋中，建築卡洗勻蓋著放置，年標記放在1965版圖右下處
*每個人拿一個顏色的30個標記，一張幫助卡，拿50000元，隨機起始
*商店板塊上數字代表要那個多間直接相鄰才會到達最大規模，版塊總數為上面數字的兩倍
*遊戲進行六輪，最後錢多的贏，每輪有六個階段
1.發建築卡  根據表格，不同人數不同輪發不同張數，跟保留不同張數(左發右保留)，棄掉跟剩下的牌洗勻蓋著放一旁，每個人根據卡片上數字放對應標記在圖板上，然後棄掉該牌
2.抽商店板塊 每個人根據表格抽右邊數字的商店板塊，所有人抽完後一起展示
3.交易階段  所有人同時討論要怎麼交易，金錢，場上建築位置，跟商店板塊都可以，直到所有人同意進入下一階段，版圖上的商店位置不能在動
4.放置商店板塊 從起始開始照順位每個人放置面前的任意商店板塊(可保留)到他的建築標記處
*商店版塊相連數目如果超過板塊上數字，多的視為另外一間，只有同玩家的同商店板塊會合併
5.收入 根據商店是否滿數目跟目前的數目領錢，錢是隱藏資訊
6.回合標記前進一格
*最後比錢，平手比場上商店數
*建議建築卡放在玩家面前同時紀錄該玩家擁有的建築點
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Trading/Set collection game, simple. Businesses are only worthwhile in sets of 3/4/5/6. There's less luck than Settlers (I think), but the trades are harder to evaluate.

You are not (simply) trading one resource card for another like Settlers, but trading various combinations of building sites, businesses to put on those sites and cash. Future promises are allowed, but non binding (so people don't try to make them, paper they're written on etc.).

After 2 games, groupthink says: it's easier to make sets of tiles than building sites; also, all things being equal, 5/6-tiles are worth more than 3/4-tiles, because ultimately that's where the big money is.

The reason I traded it away is that it plays very similarly every time. And each turn is a lot like the last. After a few games it was very easy to value everything, and from that point forwards it was clear the games were being decided by luck of the draw, so I stopped playing it.

Traded for in Summer 2010
Traded away Spring 2012

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
4-5 Players, Preferably 5. Can also play just as well at 6 with house rule



*****House Rules*****

1) Business tiles are kept face-down and secret. A player can reveal them if he wishes, but doesn’t have to.

2) Each turn a player does NOT discard two of his plot cards. Rather, he can keep through the trading round and even trade them to opponents. He has the opportunity to discard cards BEFORE refilling his hand at the beginning of the next round.

3) When receiving new plot cards at the beginning of each turn, a player receives enough cards to bring his hand back UP TO the larger number listed on the player aid card. Thus, if a player held two cards from the previous round, he would only receive three new cards in rounds 2 – 6 (in a four player game).

4) Each turn, a player is dealt a number of business tiles equal to the smaller number listed on the player aid card, regardless of how many business tiles he kept from the previous round.

*For 6 Players*
The distribution is:
5/3 5/3 4/2 4/2 4/2 2/2
and
5 3 2 2 2 1

RATING: 9
(01/18) I usually hate trading/bidding dynamics. I checked Chinatown on discount and saw it's was a lot negotiating and thought "meh" but then I read about it being part of classic games like Ra, Castles of Burgundy, etc. and did more research, I stumbled on a review that was made by the guys at SU&SD and it highlighted the incredibly free form trading mechanism this game had, and how it elevated it from a simple math game to a good interactive and specially positive interaction game. and decided to buy it (in a combo with Biblios) 

This is one of my favorite games, even if I lose, I often have a good time, the interactions feel genuine, you actually feel like you have shops on a market, rather than just putting stuff around a board. you actually feel for the stuff you build.

You win this game by having the most money, and usually it is a close victory.

RATING: 8.5
Really love that you can negotiate literally anything.

Pretty easy rules, but a lot of fun to play.

A lot of people knock the end game, but they don't understand negotiation. Everyone says it is simple math and whether things benefit them, but a great negotiator knows it's not about deals that just give you more money. If Player A thinks he is 20k ahead of B but 50 ahead of C, he would consider giving C a +30k deal that gives him nothing, just so he doesn't make a deal with b to give him anything positive.

I won x2 by taking those deals late game because the leader didn't want to take any deals that didn't profit him more than the other person. Strong negotiation is only the first part of the equation.

RATING: 8
If someone grabbed "Monopoly" and turned it into a fun game, they'd end up with "Chinatown".

I consider this one a gateway game for people who have only played the mass market games such as the aforementioned one. There's money, there's trading, there's buying/building businesses, it's pretty short (compared to, you know, "Monopoly"), no player elimination (we've seen some crazy unexpected underdog victories).

There's a luck factor involved, so your strategy will most likely change every time you play it, keeping it fresh.

RATING: 8
A free-for-all negotiation game with lots of great competition. Easy to teach, just one small paragraph of rules. It's a good companion to Acquire, which is a high compliment. 

I could think of additional rules to make it more tactical (e.g., the ability to overbuild/demolish, different maps, maybe an auction for tiles), but that would destroy the quick and cutthroat part of the game, where people build themselves into a corner. So best to have the game as it is -- more about negotiation than rules. And that aspect makes the game unique from other rule-heavy auction/trading games.

RATING: 6.5
Played once with 4P, and another with 5P. Chinatown is a free-form negotation game with a splendidly simple ruleset. Players haggle over 6 rounds for the ownership of lots and the rights to establish businesses. As with any negotiation-heavy game, the usual caveat applies that you need the [i]right group[/i] for this – play with those willing to speak up, are playing to win, yet won't drag out negotiations endlessly.

So assuming you are fortunate enough to have such a group, they also need to be into calculations (but not too much). The negotiations here are a bit too mathematical for my tastes, because while speculation on future lots and tiles means their values are not precisely calculable, players are still able to fine-tune trade values to a degree. The unevenly distributed element of speculation also leads to a few dull rounds – too little information to work with in the first round means very little can be negotiated, while the steadily decreasing randomness renders the last two rounds practically calculable.

What saves Chinatown is the inherent chaos of the free-for-all negotiations. As long as you can think on your feet and get players to agree to reasonable deals quickly, you should be able to catch number crunchers off guard. Because in the end, making the most [i]good[/i] trades trumps making one or two [i]optimum[/i] trades.

Since chaos is a good thing here, it then follows that Chinatown plays best with the maximum number of players. Sure, it will frustrate those who want complete control and order over negotiations, but I wouldn't want to play it any other way. Strengthening the case for playing this with a full table is that it lowers the odds of players drawing into a set of contiguous lots early on.

To recap, Chinatown needs a group with an uncommon combination of qualities – primarily, being willing to perform calculations yet able to tolerate some chaos. The brilliant rounds are bookended by mediocre ones, and the currency denominations in the Z-Man edition look confusingly similar. It's not the ideal negotiation game for me even in the best circumstances, but there are brief moments halfway through the game when it feels like one.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
A lightweight negotiation based game: players are dealt cards that correspond to plots of land. Negotiations are made for buidings, plots, money etc. They then build (from a random pool) buildings that score $$$ at the end of each of the six rounds.

This is the game that Traders of Genoa should be (less obtuse scoring methods, better handling of elements of negotiation). It's quick, fun and easy to get involved in. Much of the game requires players to know what things are worth, what the tile pool is like and how best to score early.

I dislike the 'random' end of turn card bonuses and they can easily give players an extra $5-15k. I say, scrap it!

RATING: 5
Solid negotiation game, although I still like Traders of Genoa much more.  Still, it's got a pure wheel-and-deal mechanism as you take what you're dealt and try to work it so that you can create something that's going to pay you out.  The game seems like it might drag, but really, the negotiation is generally simultaneous, doesn't draw out all that much, and with only six rounds, this game is not overly long and quite engaging.  In a strange way, this reminds of Monopoly in that you're trying to collect complete sets of a given property.  Of course, the lack of roll-and-move and bankrupting your opponents as an objective really raises this one quite a bit.

UPDATE: The more I think about this one, the more luck-based I think it is.  I still enjoy the negotiation aspect of it, but the card and tile draws can go a long way to cheap deals leading to victory.

UPDATE #2: Really seeing how luck comes into play with this one and just is not among my top choices anymore.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
[Alea 1999 edition] {Merchant; economic, trading, set collection, tile-laying; optimal 5p} An interesting and challenging negotiation/trading game. Among my negotiation games, Chinatown requires the heaviest negotiation skill because you can virtually negotiation any items in Chinatown. If you can't negotiate, you can never win in Chinatown. In Chinatown, any kinds of negotiation, except future promises, are allowed simultaneously. You are not bound to negotiating with an active player, or a negotiation between 2 players. A typical game runs around 1 ~ 1.5 hours, which is shorter than Diplomacy / Traders of Genoa. Since there are plenty of interactions in around an hour, it's enjoyable to play Chinatown.

There is some luck in drawing plots. The variants proposed by West Bank Gamers can be used to increase the control and depth of Chinatown. I prefer playing Chinatown with 2 variants: Bonus card is known before negotiation and new business can ONLY be built if it has access to a street.

It's sad that this game is OOP. I recommend this for those who like trading games.

[Updated] It's a good news that Chinatown will be reprinted in 2007.

RATING: 8.9
How negotiation games should work. Just enough structure to point players in the right direction, but enough freedom to do as they please.

For me, the trading and deal making in this works better than Bohnanza and I'm The Boss, though I'm The Boss is definitely funnier (just not more fun). I'm keen to play this again soon to see if it stands up to repeat plays.

Update: I'm beginning to really love this game, though it's not one I can play frequently because I find it exhausting. Of all my games, it's probably the one I want to win most every time it hits the table. Everything is for sale as a price, everything is negotiable.

I had particular fun in my last game; trading away a spot that was valuable to me to another player to give me leverage against a third player who then gave me enough money to attempt to buy back the original space I'd traded away and still leave me with a healthy profit. :D

Rating up from 7.6 to 8.9.

RATING: 6
This game is everything Monopoly wanted to be. There is no dice and it only lasts 6 rounds.

For serious gaming no way. For a gaming evening with casual gamers or family yes way. Social negotiation game. This is on the nice side, it's just business, no invading your country or other serious back stabbing involved. Luck of cards and tiles may put you in a good position without the need to negotiate, so don't take this game too seriously. It's just for fun.

Update: I prefer Genoa over Chinatown any day of the week. Last rounds of Chinatown are just too boring, because you can already calculate just about everything and Chinatown is too luck dependent for my taste. Genoa is exciting to the very end and you get the same social negotiation aspect.

RATING: 6
Here's my problem with negotiation games of this type - its technically "solvable". Here's what I mean. If you need my one space on the board to finish your business, I can see that you will make an addition [for example] 5000 each turn for the next 4 turns over what you would make without finishing. Meaning that at a minimum (there is possibility for bonuses, but I'll ignore that) giving you the space is worth 20k. Fine pay me 10k and we're good. And that's what I mean by solvable. A good player is not going to just give up money/points to another player unless the deal is equitable. Since the equality is based in math, the deals (should) be zero sum. Which means - the winner is determined randomly based on the cards that come out and the tiles you get. There is no "secret way" to change the endgame outcome - you just get lucky or play with people with no math skills...

RATING: 6
Everyone labels Chinatown a "pure" negotiation game. Negotiation is certainly front and center, but I have trouble understanding how anything can be a single "pure" mechanic. Chinatown is no different. Planning when and where to launch your new businesses is in important exercise in timing. Likewise, and probably most importantly, you have to be able to quickly and accurately calculate your expected profit for each move.

Each round you receive a random hand of cards that correspond to number squares on the board. You also receive a random hand of shop tiles, which you'll use to build up your business empire. The randomness is annoying, but probably comparable to a game like Acquire, where you might get frustrated, but you typically have options. In any case, frustration is key to making the game work because it forces you to make trades with other players. This means you'll want deeds and shop tiles that are either really helpful to you, or really helpful to someone else. Middling tiles are nearly useless.

After you receive your deeds and tiles, the game opens up for a free-for-all negotiation phase. If that sound daunting, don't worry. Despite the open-ended feel, there are typically only a few things you're looking to get done. And because information about everyone else's holdings (except money) is open, it's quick and easy to assess who you need to talk to, and what they might be interested in. I'd say the level of negotiation is only slight more involved than [thing=11][/thing], for example. The real trick is trying to understand how much you should pay (or demand) for each deal because there is a lot to consider.

And therein lies both the strength and the weakness of Chinatown. You'll likely have a strong desire to come up with some sort of evaluation for every tile placement, every deed, and of course every negotiation. The number (and complexity) of calculations you should probably be performing is astronomical, but the game is much more fun when everyone is just sort of eyeballing it all. Experience will help. Most importantly, everyone needs to work together to make sure no single player gets out to an early lead. These things are easy enough to keep an eye on. The more elaborate trades, especially late in the game, are where things get tricky.

Overall, Chinatown is a solid game that I'd gladly play again. The only thing giving me pause is that evaluation problem. I know I could be playing a more mathematically sound strategy, but I know shouldn't do that because it will bog the game down and kill the fun. In other words, it's a game that fights against its own nature -- but I probably just need to get over myself and have fun.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6
The game was exactly what it was billed as, a pure trading game.  In fact it is almost a complete sandbox.  You get some properties and some industries and then you are open to trade anything you want, any kind of deals you want to make.  

There are virtually no rules that I can see for trading, and the book literally says you may trade anything.  So promises, lies, residual payments, etc are all on the table.

Then you build out the stuff you acquired and make money, rinse and repeat.

The game requires all the players to want to be involved in and trade, this is not a game where you can sort of refuse to be involved in the trading aspect, as you will have no fun and you will lose.

The game also lends itself to some kingmaking because a smooth talker can just get awesome deals for themselves, but I think that is the point of the design.

RATING: 8.9
If you like games with trading, wheeling, and dealing, then this is a game you should really try. Each round players draw plots of land. Then players draw shop tiles, but leave them in front of themselves. At this point it is time to make trades. Everything is up for grabs trade wise. Only shop tiles already built cannot be moved, otherwise claimed plots of land can be swapped at will. Owned shops are up for negotiations too. My group loves this game. I bought it in the sale bin last October, and within a week 4 more people in the group bought it. That is a mark of a good game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
This could be fun but I think there are some alarming issues. If someone wants to buy something from you, you can calculate what they will gain from having it and ask for some percentage of it (any percentage you can think of). Often there is no one else to sell the same thing (especially if it's land rather than a placeable business) so there's no competition to keep prices reasonable. Sort of the opposite problem of Bohnanza, in that there are too many possible deals. I don't think it's that interesting to see how much you can extort from someone.

Interesting to think of how to handle the early game situation where the buyer doesn't have on-hand a reasonable percentage of the worth of what he wants to buy. Maybe a deal could last into future turns (but be non-binding).

Getting screwed by the tile draw seems like a real issue. If everyone has the same deal-making ability I guess the rest is just luck, because I don't see much non-obvious strategy...

The last turn of the game seems to completely suck as there are only useless plots of land still in the deck for the most part. People just empty their hand to get a few more bucks out of their useless holdings.

Trackable money, theoretically secret, is a potential issue too. If people play mainly for first place and have an idea about who's in the lead, the game could break and have kingmaker situations.

RATING: 9
This is a negotiation game in the truest sense of the word. In it, players acquire ownership of sections of city blocks then place tiles, representing businesses, onto the block-sections. At the end of each turn, each tile you've laid gives you some sort of payout, but completed businesses (formed of three to six connected tiles of the same type) pay quite a bit better. All these resources are dealt to the players randomly, however, so players must trade to get matching businesses and adjacent locations.

RATING: 7
A pretty sweet trading game with straightforward mechanics along the lines of "I want what you have, you want what I have, let's make an equitable trade." Except of course, you are constantly trying to swing the trade in your favor. Players try to negotiate a purchase or trade for shop lots and businesses in an effort to establish a chain or cluster of shop lots operating a single type of business. Success means expanding your business and bringing in more revenue each round. A really simple yet intense trading game. Not for folks who don't like negotiation games.

RATING: 6
Trading game set in New York's Chinatown. Incorporates open trading for numbered lots, business tiles, built businesses and money - good play involves creativity in the trading phase, which is the meat of the game. When played with the standard rules, seems to involve a run-away leader problem if certain players are able to build decent businesses in the early turns while others cannot - this is mainly an issue as that capacity is essentially dictated by the early lot and tile draw. There is also an issue where the open information may be prohibitive to trading as players can see the benefits of certain trades to others. I may like to try this with the "West Bank" variant introduced in these forums, which may correct some of these issues by adding some hidden information. Despite the issues, Chinatown shows promise as the game is very interactive, simple and has an original theme - new players generally "get it" after one round. As for the components (Filosofia/Z-man) - (1) unlike the droves on BGG that seem to overwhelmingly shun paper money, I would have preferred that here instead of the small hard to differentiate green money cards; (2) the board is beautiful but my copy is prone to warping; (3) the reference cards are a little small; (4) otherwise, the art, the wood disks and business tiles are excellent quality and the game includes the necessary draw bag for the tiles.
