RATING: 6
Essayer.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
I love negotiation games. My love for the genre is one part nostalgia (too much monopoly as a kid-- where fearsome, wild trading is the only enjoyable memory from those many wasted hours) mixed with the overly romantic ideal of mastering the artful deal. And so it makes sense that I'd like Chinatown. But, like so many loves rooted in the romantic, a more sober look reveals that the object of desire is greatly flawed. And like so many loves rooted in the romantic, that hardly matters!

Chinatown is great! Chinatown is terrible! Both of these statements are true I am happy/sorry to report.

Chinatown is great! As a romp through the rush of good deal making Chinatown is great. The rules are so very extremely simple (the game works for gamers and non-gamers!), the game length just right and the complexity of the possible deals rich enough to  allow for real creativity. ("Hey! I'll trade money and tiles for property spaces and an established business over there") As two hours spent trying to get an edge with a few inspired deals this is a blast.

Chinatown is terrible! Truly crafty, artful deal making relies on asymmetric information and asymmetric value. Sadly, Chinatown lacks asymetric information which reduces any positive deal outcome to either: (a) coming out ahead via luck or (b) duping an opponent by being better at the (simple) maths.

In Chinatown, the precise value of most deals-- and all late game deals-- is calculable and known to all players. If everyone can do the math we'd expect that optimum play would produce zero sum trades. If optimum play produces zero sum trades then the winner will be determined by the luck of the draw-- who pulls that last factory they need out of the tile bag or the 5th property space card right next to their adjacent four.

The only way for a negotiation game to avoid this trap is to include asymmetric information: I know something about the future value that you do not which leads to asymmetric value: the value to me is different than it is to you because of how I can use what I get in combination with things I already have and know. A good example of a game that has these elements, and thus an example of a superior trading game is Traders of Genoa.

It is interesting to note that the geek fix to this "Chinatown Problem" is to introduce asymmetric information by making property locations closed until the end of the round. (In the rules as written this is open information.) Many claim this introduction of asymmetric information completely "fixes" the game. (See the "Westbank Gamers Variant" thread for more.)

OK. So there is the fatal flaw of Chinatown: that the value of all deals are known to all in advance. This essential problem is spelled out in many of the critical game comments here on BGG. But yet, like real life trading, just because something has the elements required of a zero-sum trade, doesn't mean that it will be one automatically. Why? Because even if the particular concrete elements of the trade are calculable, not all players will calculate well or even calculate at all-- and this is what saves Chinatown: the asymmetrical information isn't in the conjunction of the players' hands and board state, rather it is in knowing which player is the sucker and how to get what you want from him/her.

In the words of some great anonymous poker player (or Chinatown player?), "If you don't know who the sucker at the table is, it is probably you." What emerges in (I imagine) most games of Chinatown is that someone at the table is more willing to give favorable deals-- which is a nice way of saying there is likely to be a sucker at the table. And what is known to different degrees by the other players is who that player is, and what type of negotiation (hard ball? soft sell? bestest-friends-foreverish?) is needed in order to be their primary trading partner. Chinatown is saved from its own fatal flaw: the asymmetric information is: "who is the sucker?"; the asymmetric value is: "how do I present a trade offer from my portfolio that maximizes my trading with the sucker?"

So, if every player at your table is a hard-core number chomping robot, this game will come down to luck of the draw. But if even one single player is making deals by gut, the flaw is fixed and gaming the player's at the table is pure negotiating bliss. Bring me a table with at least one sucker, and I'll show you two hours of electrifying gaming delight.

RATING: N/A
New York in the 1960’s. A new wave of Chinese immigrants is moving into Chinatown. The adoption of the new immigration act has launched the district in a demographic boom! It now reaches Canal Street to the north and Bowery Street to the east. The immigrants, hard-working men and women, are arriving by the thousands to buy buildings, establish businesses and fulfill the American Dream! In this game, you are one of them. With all your savings in hand, the Big Apple is up for grabs! Will you know how to use your talents and acquire the most extraordinary fortune in America?
\13\\13\
The board represents the New York Chinatown of the mid-60’s. It is divided into 6 districts containing buildings numbered from 1 to 85. A game is played over 6 rounds. In each round, the players receive income from the businesses they have managed to establish. To generate the maximum income, shops of the same type need to be built on adjacent buildings. At the beginning of each round, players draw new Building cards and new Shop tiles. They must then use their negotiation skills to acquire adjacent buildings and establish businesses. But it is not always that easy...
\13\\13\
In this game where everything can be negotiated, only the hard laws of the market prevail.

RATING: 7
This is probably the most free form game in my collection. Each player gets a random set of business tiles, a random set of property spaces and then is let loose to make as many deals as possible to try and turn those locations into money. Anything goes, the player that has the most money at the end of five rounds wins.

It's seems much too straight-forward to be any fun, yet I've enjoyed every session. My only concern is that once everyone is comfortable with the game, the trading will become predictable. I have the Z-man version, so there are no random payout cards to mix things up. I wish I knew if these helped the game, because if they make it better or prevent the game from getting stale, then it would certainly be worth it to proxy some up myself.

RATING: 7.5

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 1
Imagine a simpler (lol) version of Ra with auctioning replaced by free-for-all trading of tiles, except the lucksackery is squared because players randomly draw not only the set-collection business tiles but also the locations in which they're placed. (Contiguous placement required, the numbering system on the board recalls Acquire here.) Players can suffer shortages if tiles from the set they're collecting aren't pulled, arbitrarily causing loss of income provided that they were fortunate enough to have already accumulated the needed build space, but the bag contains more copies than just the complete set so players are more frequently screwed by needing area to build their already assembled sets of businesses. Lucking into numbers that border is crucial, and the process takes multiple rounds. If the numbers you need are never drawn, kept, or opponents hold out because you have nothing (and cannot obtain something) to offer them, expect to lose income for each round you're tardy. Players can haggle on price but the information is perfect - you know exactly how much the benefit is worth and for how many rounds - so I would imagine a few plays might even codify the numbers.

Or think of it another way: trading is mutually beneficial to the parties involved, meaning their position improves relative to everyone not involved. Thus, the person that trades most often has the greatest chance of winning. To be able to trade most often, you must have the most to offer. Having the most to offer requires drawing all the best business tiles and building cards. See the dilemma?
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Another gorgeous Alea big box game.  The artwork is perfect and overall quality of components is what you'd expect from Alea.  The game itself is good, but seems pretty stripped down.  If you want pure negotiation, this one is excellent.  But there are other games out there that have a real game going on in addition to the ability to argue with your friends.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A trading / negotiation game in the truest sense.  There is a little luck in drawing land plots or shop tiles that you either need or are trade-able for what you need.  Negotiation games take a knack for coming up with a deal that is mutually beneficial.  Some people have a good feel for this, and others don't.  Playing this type of game with the right group of people is paramount.

This one has a good deal of angst in making the deals. Do you make the deal or wait a turn and see if you draw the plot or tile you need? There is a good balance there that makes this an exciting and fun game.

RATING: 8
It turns out I really like trading games!  The first time I explained the rules (draw cards for lots, draw tiles for businesses, trade anything, make money from businesses, rinse and repeat...) we felt a bit leary of something so simple. I'm still suprised how much we laughed during that game! We got heavily into the number crunching near the end (but the early uncertainty was probably more fun).  I'm not sure how I would have felt about what I got in the box if I had to pay the full MSRP... but what you get is a very attractive game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
A word of warning to others.  If you have the tendency to think people are out to get you or to take things very personal, do not play negotiation games like this.  With that said, I realize the haggling aspect is central to Chinatown (which I suppose is a fairly accurate reflection of my culture's way of buying and selling goods), but I can't say I enjoy it very much.  Prime real estate locations and shop tiles you can bargain with are valuable to have, but it is equally important to not to push your luck too much and learn to compromise because no resources are 100% definite, unless it happens to be the very last one available.  Bottomline is Chinatown has far too many random elements for me to truly call the experience fun.

RATING: 8
Rating after one play. Very good negotiation game. I just love these game where you need to rave like a madman to accomplish thing. But still, this gave me the experience I expected from Genoa and I think Chinatown succeeded in the attempt little bit better than the big G.

RATING: 5
After 1 Play: I don't have much experience with negotiation games, but I enjoyed my first play of this.  It took a few turns to get going, but after that some interesting deals were made.  I'm hoping to try Traders of Genoa soon, so after that I'll have something to compare this to.

After 2+ Plays: I liked this game significantly less after playing it again.  The real problem is the transparency of what each item is worth to each player, which make the negotiations a lot less interesting.  Moved rating from 7 to 5.

RATING: 5
Of the trading games I've played, this is one of my favorites because it allows you to be creative with your trades, encourages interactivity, yet doesn't bog down or devolve into pointless discussions (I'm looking at you, Traders of Genoa).

Easy recommendation for those that need a high social factor that isn't just take-that action cards or something.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
1/1/12 -- Chinatown and I didn't ultimately work out, so I filed the official divorce papers.  I always wondered why RGG never printed the Alea version of this, and maybe now I know why.

11/30/08 -- I don't feel my first two games have adequately shown this game's depth.  It seems like there is more room for insidious and layered deal-making that we haven't quite gotten to yet, which means I'm looking forward to further exploring the game.  I like that it's quick and simple (although Quo Vadis? still has it beat).  Playing the trading phase with a time-limit of 5-7 minutes seems like a good idea (since late-game trading phases started taking a long time while people tried to eke out every last dollar from their trades) and is something I'll attempt to try out next time we play.
